Station\nPhase B Exten sion Period Study (Contract NAS8- 25140).\n\nThe purpo se of the\n\nextension period has been to develop the Phase B definition of the Modular\nSpace Station.\n\nThe modular approach selected during the option period\n\n(characterized by low initial co st and inc remental manning) was evaluated,\nrequirements were defined, and program definition and design were accomplished to the depth necessary for departure from Phase B.\n\nThe initial 2-1 /2-month effort of the extension period was used for analyses\nof the requirements associated with Modular Space Station Program options.\nDuring this time, a baseline, incrementally manned program and attendant\nexperiment program options were derived.\n\nIn addition, the features of the\n\nprogram that significantly affect initial development and early operating costs\nwere identified, and their impacts on the program were assessed.\n\nThis\n\nas ses sment, together with a recommended program, was submitted for NASA\nreview and approval on 15 April 1971.\n\nThe second phase of the study (15 April to 3 December 1971) consists of the\nprogram definition and preliminary design of the approved Modular Space\nStation configuration.\n\nA subject reference matrix is included on page v to indicate the relationship\nof the study tasks to the documentation.\n\nThis report is submitted as Data Requirement MP-02.\n\nIII\n\nDATA REQUIREMENTS (DR\'s)\nMSFC-DPD-235/DR NOs.\n(contract NAS8-25140)\n\nCategory\n\nDe signation\n\nDR\nNumber\n\nConfiguration\n\nCM\n\nCM-Ol\n\nTitle\nSpace Station Shuttle in the late 1970\xc2\xb7 s, a long-term manned\nscientific laboratory in Earth orbit will become feasible.\n\nUsing the shuttle\n\nfor orbital buildup, logistics delivery, and return of scientific data, this\nlaboratory will provide many advantages to the scientific community and will\nmake available to the United States a platform for application to the solution\nof national problems such as ecology research, weather observation and\nprediction, and research in medicine and the life sciences.\n\nIt will be ideally\n\nsituated for Earth and space observation, and its location above the atmosphere will be of great benefit to the field of astronomy.\n\nThis orbiting laboratory can take many forms and can be configured to house\na crew of up to 12 men.\n\nThe initial study of the 33-foot-diameter Space\n\nStation, launched by the Saturn INT-2l and supporting a complement of 12,\nhas been completed to a Phase B level and documented in the DRL-160 series.\nRecently completed studie s are centered around a Space Station comprised of\nsmaller, shuttle-launched modules.\n\nThese modules could ultimately be con-\n\nfigured to provide for a crew of the same size as on the 33-foot-diameter\nSpace Station-but buildup would be gradual, beginning with a small initial\ncrew and progressing toward greater capability by adding modules and\ncrewmen on a flexible schedule.\n\nThe Modular Space Station Phase A-level study results are documented in the\nDRL-23l series.\n\nRecent Modular Space Station Phase B study results are\n\ndocumented in the DPD-235 series, of which this is a volume.\n\nThe Space Station will provide laboratory areas which, like similar facilities\non Earth, will be designed for flexible, efficient changeover as research and\nexperimental programs proceed.\n\nProvisions will be included for such\n\nfunctions as data processing and evaluation, astronomy support, and test and\n1\n\ncalibration of optics.\n\nZero gravity, which is desirable for the conduct of\n\nexperiments, will be the normal mode of operation.\n\nIn addition to experi-\n\nments carried out within the station, the laboratorie s will support operation\nof experiments in separate modules that are either docked to the Space\nStation or free-flying.\n\nFollowing launch and activation, Space Station operations will be largely\nautonomous, and an extensive ground support complex will be unnecessary.\nGround activitie swill\n\n0\n\nrdinarily be limited to long- range planning, control\n\nof logistics, and support of the experiment program.\n\nThe Initial Space Station (ISS) will be delivered to orbit by three Space Shuttle\nlaunches and will be assembled in space.\n\nA crew in the Shuttle orbiter will\n\naccompany the module s to as semble them and check interfacing functions.\n\nISS resupply and crew rotation will be carried out via round-trip Shuttle\nflights using Logistics Modules (Log M 1 s) for transport and on-orbit storage\nof cargo.\n\nOf the four Log M\'s required, one will remain on orbit at all times.\n\nExperiment modules will be delivered to the Space Station by the Shuttle as\nrequired by the experiment program.\n\nOn return flights, the Shuttle will\n\ntransport data from the experiment program, returning crewmen, and\nwaste s.\n\nThe ISS configuration rendering is shown in the frontispiece.\n\nThe Power /\n\nSubsystems Module will be launched first, followed at 30-day intervals by the\nCrew /Operations Module and the General Purpose Laboratory (GPL) Module.\nThis configuration will provide for a crew of six.\n\nSubsequently, two addi-\n\ntional modules (duplicate Crew/Operations and Power/Subsystems Modules)\nwill be mated to the ISS to form the Growth Space Station (GSS) (shown in the\nfrontispiece), which will house a crew of 12 and provide a capability equivalent to the 33-foot INT-2l-launched Space Station.\n\nGSS logistics support will\n\nuse a Crew Cargo Module capable of transporting a crew of six.\n\n2\n\nDuring ISS operations, five research applications modules (RAM\'s) will be\nassembled to the Space Station.\ncompletion of the GSS.\n\nThree of these will be returned prior to\n\nIn the GSS configuration, 12 additional RAMI swill\n\naugment the two remaining from the ISS phase.\n\nThree of the RAM\'s\n\ndelivered to the GSS will be free-flying modules.\n\n1, 2 SCOPE OF THIS VOLUME\n\nThis volume describes the requirements for the activities involved in, and\nthe procedures used by the crew in the operations of the Modular Space\nStation.\n\nFor completeness and clarity, a description of all crew-related\n\ncharacteristics of the statio"n and its operations are indicated.\n\nSection 2 describes the interior configuration and arrangement of each of the\nSpace Station module s, the facilitie s and equipment in the module and their\noperation, as related to crew habitability.\n\nSection 3 defines the crew activities and procedures involved in the operation\nof the station in the accomplishment of its pr,imary mis sion.\n\nIt de scribe s\n\nthe operations involved in initial station buildup; and the on-orbit operation\nand maintenance of the station and its subsystems to support the experimental program.\n\nA general description of experiment operations is also\n\ngiven.\nA detailed description of experiment operations at the functional program\nelement level is presented in Appendix A, prepared by the Martin Marietta\nCorporation, Denver Division.\n\n3\n\nSection 2\nCREW FACILITIES AND OPERATIONS\n\n2.1\n\nMODULAR SPACE STATION CONCEPT\n\nThe fundamental design philosophies which have shaped the Modular Space\nStation concept are (1) low cost through simplicity and (2) effectiveness\nthrough modern technology.\n\nThe first objective is achieved by limiting the\n\nnumber of basic modules with multifunction elements.\n\nThe second helps to\n\nreduce not only the amount of structure but also the thermal control, safety\nand warning, fault isolation, electrical and fluid distribution systems.\nadditional module also requires another docking interface.\n\nEach\n\nWhile the Initial\n\nSpace Station employs common structural and thermal control system elements\' and other common elements in the individual module designs, the\nprimary simplification and cost reduction due to commonality is in the\ngrowth from a 6-man to a l2-n.an station by the addition of a power subsystem module and a crew operation module, both identical to the original\nISS development.\n\nA completely maintainable design was developed.\n\nEach\n\nsubsystem design incorporates its own maintainability features which result\nin a greatly simplified total program (including development and operations).\n\nIncreased system effectiveness is achieved through the automation of station\nfacilities to reduce nonproductive man-hours (e. g., fault isolation, failure,\nwarning, and subsystem control).\n\nThe general-purpose laboratory is\n\ndesigned to provide the maximum involvement of man in the research and\napplication activities.\n\nThe ISS is capable of supporting a crew of up to six men.\nconfiguration consists of three module s:\n\nThe basic station\n\n(1) a Power / Subsystem Module\n\nillustrated with deployed solar arrays and containing all power distribution\nequipment and other subsystems; (2) a Crew / Operations Module capable of\nhousing up to six men and containing the station command center (attached\nlongitudinally to the Power / Subsystems Module); and (3) a radially attached\n\n5\n\nGPL, which includes a biotechnology facility as well as laboratories for\ngene ral re search and applications support.\n\nBoth the Powe r / Subsystems and\n\nthe crew modules have three lateral docking ports for the accommodation of\nthe GPL and RAM\'s, while the end port on the docking module is nominally\nused for docking the logistic s module.\n\nTo achieve GSS capability, the two additional Space Station modules (Power/\nSubsystems and Crew/Operation) will be added to the ISS cluster.\n\nThese\n\nmodules are nearly identical in design with those deployed for the initial\ncapability.\n\nThese two modules double the capability of the Space Station and\n\nenable it to perform all functions required for the GSS.\n\nGSS buildup increases the crew size from six to 12 men, and doubles the\npower, docking ports, etc.\n\nThe initial GPL is sized for the GSS capability\n\nand off-loaded for the ISS; therefore, only one GPL is required.\n\nSpecial\n\ncrewmen are again employed with delivery of the GSS modules to assist the\nISS crew in activating the GSS.\n\nManning to the 12-man level is accomplished\n\nby use of a second Crew Cargo Module.\n\n2.2\n\nMAN-SYSTEM INTEGRATION\n\nThe interior configuration of all the Space Station modules has been designed\nto facilitate crew operations and hence habitability.\n\nThe major program\n\nrequirements specified by NASA were as follows:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nNo artificial g.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDual, independent pre s sure volume s.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nProvide equivalent provisions as 33-ft Station.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPrivate state rooms.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nProvide for ease of accessibility for maintenance\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nProvide alternate escape routes.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n96-hr emergency capability.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nProvide emergency EVA/IVA suit station.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nConsistent with good architectural de sign.\n\n6\n\nTo aITlplify and clarify the intent of the se prograITl requireITlents, the following\ngeneral requireITlents or design goals were generated by McDonnell Douglas\nAstronautics COITlpany (MDAC) through design analyses to guide the preliITlinary de sign effo rt:\nA.\n\nAll cOITlpartITlents will be designed for ITlaxiITluITl habitability.\n\nB.\n\nInteriors will be optiITlized with due consideration to ground\ncheckout and evaluation requirements and constraints.\n\nC.\n\nVolume allocation, equipment locations, and interior arrangement\nwill be designed to minimize casual interference, (i, e., to avoid\nany interuption to crewmen while performing tasks).\n\nD.\n\nThe opportunity for freedom of choice in facility usage will be\nmaximiz ed.\n\nE.\n\nThe common use of free space will be applied where appropriate.\n\nThe general interior orientation and configuration selected is essentially the\nsame for all module s, although they vary in detail according to the specific\nrequirements of each module.\n\nThe selected orientation and configuration can\n\nbe described as a longitudinal orientation, using a minimum of separating\nwalls or decks, but with arrangement of equipment and facilities for two\nlevels of operation where po s sible.\n\nEach facility work station has been sized (shape and volume) to provide sufficient free space to ensure oprimum task performance or satisfaction of\nneeds, minimum interference from other crew members or equipITlent,\nmultiple use of facilitie s without routine scheduling, and a maximum number\nof alternative areas for accomplishing specific job requirements (e. g., study\nor report preparation) or meeting personal needs (e. g., social activities or\nprivacy).\n\nThe location and arrangement of facilities and equipment facilitates\n\nthe above capability, and minimize s the total Space Station volume requirements; while at the same tiITle maximizing the appearance of spaciousness\nand the COD1mon use of free space, and facilitates the accoITlD1.odation of ITlixecl\ncrews (male and female,\n\nscientists and astronauts) and dual- shift operations.\n\nThe concurrent development of interior 3\n\nLOGISTICS MODULE\n\nThe Logistics Module is a l4-ft-dia by 28-ft-long configuration,\nincorporating a pressurized and an unpressurized compartment.\n\nthe interior\n\nof the pressurized compartment is arranged into three basic areas:\ncargo, palletized cargo, and liquid and gas cargo.\n\nspecial\n\nThe special cargo area\n\nis sized to accept off-loaded items such as the CMG!s, food freezer, trash\ncompactor, and research and applications equipment, as shown in Figure 2-31.\n\nThe palletized area is configured to support 2 ft by 2 ft carry-on\n\ncontainers.\n\nCarry-on packages are designed to accommodate this capability\n\ninsofar as po s sible.\n\n(The unpre ssurized area house s the propellant cargo\n\ntankage and high- pre s sure gaseous nitrogen tanks and need not be acce ssed\nby the crew.)\n\nTankage for fluid cargo remains fixed in the module while the\n\ncontents are transferred to station systems on demand.\n\nA two -man EVA airlock is located at the interface of the pre s surized compartment and orbiter.\n\nThe airlock also serves as a means of egress and\n\ningress between the orbiter and the Logistics Module.\n\nCargo handling aids\n\nare provided to mechanically assist crewmen in off-loading large-mass\nitems (more than 200 lb) which are easy to move but difficult to control.\nGenerally, cargo remains stowed in the Logistics Module until needed, or\nuntil the Logistic s Module is replaced.\n\n2.4\n\nBASELINE SUBSYSTEMS\n\nBaseline subsystems characteristics are summarized in Table 2-1.\n\nThe\n\noperation and maintenance of tasks\n\nhave been identified and further that the most up-to-date data are made\navailable.\n\nA brief summary of each subsystem follows.\n\nElectrical power for the Initial Space Station at 115 vdc is provided by a\ngimbaled foldout solar array.\nfor\n\nA solar heat collector, which provides heat\n\nEci LS processes, is located on the array to take advantage of the sun\n\norientation.\n\nThermal control is provided by active, redundant radiator loops\n\non each module.\n\nThe external fluid is Freon- 21 and the internal heat con-\n\nduction fluid is water.\n46\n\n.\nFigure 231 . 3.\n\nIt oper-\n\nates with the data management system computer and has the functional capabilities of the control and display console scheduled for delivery in the Crew/\nOperations Module, but it operates in a manual, one-command-at-a-time,\nmode.\n\nThe PDCU can thus be used for command activities and for diagnostic\n\nroutine s for fault detection and isolation.\n\nFollowing the se activitie s, the crew\n\nwill return to the Shuttle, doff suits, and complete food and wa ste\nmanagement.\nThe crew will then reenter the Power / Subsystems Module in shirtsleeve sand\ncomplete the checkout operations.\n\nThe first activity is the deployment of the\n\nsolar array system (see Figure 3-4).\n\nOnce the array support beams are in\n\nplace and held by removable over-center locks, the twelve array panels will\nbe deployed by means of the Astromast assembly.\nv\n\nThis assembly urn\n\n"~sh;\n\nof\n\ncollapsed trus s beam which snaps into place one section at a Li.me, the I\', ; \'y\n\nextending the sections ahead of it.\nOnce the arrays are deployed, the crew will check the power generation capability of the system and then switch the power source to the arrays.\n\n54\n\nThe\n\nFigure 3-3. ori-\n\nentation control system will be activated and checked out.\n\nProper system\n\nresponse and panel operation can be evaluated by the crew while the Space\nShuttle maintains orbital rate of the total configuration.\nWith the module on solar array power, the assembly crew will begin a communication te st on the VHF and S- Band systems with the ground network.\nWhile one crewman is performing these tests, the second crewman begins the\nnext activation sequence for the high-thrust, N2H4 propulsion system.\n\nThe\n\npropulsion system will be checked by the crewman by means of a series of\ncontrolled tests that are repeated several times to accumulate a reasonable\namount of operating time in the space environment.\n\nWith verification of this\n\n\'system operation, the system is deactivated and the initial certification of the\nmodule operational readiness is attained.\n\nThe crew will return to the\n\nShuttle, debrief with mission operations support on the ground, and prepare\nfor a sleep cycle onboard the Shuttle.\nOn the second day, the crew will reenter the Power/Subsystem Module to\ncheck the horizon sensor and star tracker systems.\n\nOnce this check is com-\n\npleted, the Space Shuttle will return to its normal orientation.\n\nThe crew will\n\nthen perform periodic tests on the module! s subsystems, similar to a\n"shakedown cruise.\n\n11\n\nFollowing 24 hours of shakedown cruise, the crew will prepare for release of\nthe module and return of the Space Shuttle.\n\nThis operation will begin once the\n\non-orbit crew and the ground support personnel have established the module\'s\nreadiness for 60 days of unmanned operations.\n\nThe Power/Subsystems mod-\n\nule will be configured for unmanned operations by activation of the atmosphere supply onboard the module.\n\nThe crew will then transfer to the payload\n\ntunnel area and secure the module hatch.\n\nFollowing this, the crew will dis-\n\nconnect the umbilicals across the interface and then enter the Space Shuttle\nairl?ck, securing the tunnel hatch.\n\nThe tunnel will then be depressurized and\n\nthe Shuttle will orient the module to its separation attitude.\n\nOnce the desired\n\nattitude has been attained, the Space Shuttle will separate from the\n\n57\n\nPower / Subsystems module.\n\nFollowing separation, the module propulsion\n\nsystem will"be activated and the hatch cover closed over the exposed docking\nport by RF command from the Space Shuttle.\n\nFor one orbit or more, the\n\nShuttle will station-keep, with the module monitoring proper subsystem operation.\n\nAt the proper time, the Space Shuttle, with the assembly crewmen\n\nonboard, will initiate the retrofiring for return to Earth and completion of\nthe first phase of the buildup operations.\n3. 1. 2\n\nCrew/Operations Module\n\nThe second module launched will be the Crew/Operations Module.\n\nOnce the\n\nCrew / Operations Module is delivered to orbit and docked to the longitudinal\ndocking port of the Power/Subsystems Module, 36 interfaces will be mated\nbetween the two modules.\n\nTo perform the operations of mating these\n\nmodule-to-module interfaces, two assembly crewmen will accompany the\nCrew/Operations Module to orbit as Space Shuttle passengers.\nThis second flight of the buildup operations also includes the first direct.\ndocking operations of one module to another, utilizing the Space Shuttle systems.\n\nThe technique for direct docking utilizes manual control by a Space\n\nShuttle crewman located at the docking station in the Space Shuttle airlock.\nThe docking aid is a T - bar device located above the target docking hatch\nand, when viewed through the Space Shuttle docking tele scope, its image\nyields information relative to lateral and vertical displacement of the Space\nShuttle.\n\nThe docking mechanism consists of a docking collar with two pro-\n\ntruding guide arms on one module, and an identical collar on the other module with two guide arms in opposite corners to provide a neuter type of\narrangement.\n\nThe guide arms force alignment in the lateral, vertical, and\n\nroll positions during final closure. At closure, capture links located on the\nguide arms hold the collars together while the collars are retracted by collapsing their hydraulic energy-absorbing members.\n\nWhen the two mating\n\nsurfaces are in contact, 24 interdigitated hooks (12 on each module) are\nengaged, and redundant seals are pre s surized to seal the docking interface.\n\n58\n\nFigure 3-5 presents t?-e buildup timelines for the crew and operations\nmodule delivery mission.\n\nAs shown in the figure, at a ground elapsed time\n\nof approximately five hours, the Space Shuttle will have completed rendezvous with the Power /Subsystems Module and the Shuttle bay door will be\nopened and the module deployed fo r docking.\n\nAs during the Power /\n\nSubsystems Module deliver mis sion, the freon loop of the Crew/Operations\nModule will be activated following module deployment from the Shuttle payload bay.\n\nDuring the ascent of the Space Shuttle, the mission operations\n\nsupport personnel have completed their operational commands to the target\nmodule, activating the rendezvous and docking aids and deploying the hatch\ncover over the target. docking port.\n\nBefore initiating the terminal docking maneuver, the high-gain antenna\nlocated on the Crew /Operations Module must be rotated to the docking orientation.\n\nThis orientation eliminates any interference with the docking\n\noperation, including the docking pilot \' s view.\n\nAlso, the RF link between the\n\nSpace Shuttle and the Power / Subsystems Module will be verified, as it is\nrequired for the nominal docking operation.\n\nAt a ground elapsed time of approximately six hours, the docking operation\nwill be completed.\n\nImmediately following docking completion, the Space\n\nStation attitude control system will be deactivated and the orbiting configuration attitude orientation will be controlled by the Space Shuttle until\ncompletion of the on-orbit activities.\n\nOnce this system has been deactivated,\n\nthe crew will transfer to the Shuttle airlock and enter the crew transfer tunnel.\n\nOnce the two assembly crewmen arrive at the entry hatch into the\n\nCrew /Operations Module, they will activate the module lighting and intercom systems and perform visual checks to establish the habitability of the\nmodule.\n\nThe Crew /Operations module contains the atmosphere control system\nand sufficient oxygen and nitrogen for l2-man days of habitation; however,\nthis will be kept in reserve and the module(s) will be supported by the Space\nShuttle environmental control system.\n\n59\n\nO\'l\n\no\n\nG.E.T.(HRS)\n\n5\n\nCREWMAN NO. perform a\nfood and sleep cycle.\nThe second day, the crew will be briefed by the support personnel on the\nground to identify any anomalies which occurred during their sleep cycle, and\nthen reenter the Crew/Operations Module to continue the buildup operations.\nOne of the first activities to be completed on the second day is the mating of\nthe hydrazine propulsion lines.\n\nSince NZH4 is considered a hazardous fluid,\n\nit is transferred inside an evacuated sleeve.\n\nThe interface surfaces of both\n\nthe fluid line and the outer sleeve are joined by appropriate means (bolts,\nV-band clamps, etc.).\n\nThe outer sleeve can be retracted for ease of con-\n\nnection of the inner line.\n\nOn completion of the interface mating, the crew\n\nwill return to the Crew /Operations Module and secure the hatch, isolating\nthe interface volume from both modules.\n\nBy proper valve manipulation,\n\nthese lines will then be evacuated, and the propellant lines filled from the\nsource tanks in the Power/Subsystems Module.\nThe high-gain antennas will then be deployed to their normal position, from\nthe stowed po sition utilized during docking.\n\nFollowing this operation, the\n\ncrewmen I- - - -..\nL\n:\':1----:\'5~:------:5!:-2\nG.E.T. (HRSI\n\nFigure 3-9. Buildup Timeline-Logistics Module\n\n74\n\n-:~\n\n\'16\n\n12\'\n\nAfter a briefing with the ground support personnel, the crew will transfer to\nthe Log M to complete the inte rface mating required between the logistic s\nvehicle and the Space Station.\n\nOnce the interface operations have been com-\n\npleted, the crew will begin transfer of equipment which has been off-loaded\nfrom the Space Station modules for launch.\nFollowing the transfer and installation of the off-loaded items, the newly\ninstalled equipment will be checked out and its operability verified.\n\nAt\n\napproximately 75 hours GET the crew will utilize the Space Station for their\nfood, hygiene, and sleep cycles.\n\nApproximately 93 hours following liftoff, the\n\nSpace Shuttle will separate from the Space Station, leaving the two crewmen\nonboard the Space Station to begin nominal operations.\n\nAfter station-keeping\n\nfor approximately one day on-orbit, the Space Shuttle will return to earth.\nA total of three logistic s mis sions is required to complete the activation\noperations and build the on-orbit crew to a six-man level.\n\nThese flights will\n\nbe 30 days apart, resulting in completion of buildup and activation five months\nafter the first Space Station launch.\n\nDuring the buildup and activation oper-\n\nations, a crew duty-cycle day of 20 hours was utilized.\n\nThe short duration\n\nof the missions indicated that the crew did not require the nominal fourhour recreation period each day, therefore, none were scheduled.\n\nThis\n\napproach enables greater utilization of the crew time on-orbit; however, during sustained operations, with the crew on-orbit for extended periods, the\nrecreation and relaxation periods should be and have been included in the\ndaily schedule s.\n3.2 ORBITAL OPERA TIONS\nThe sustained operations of the Space Station for crew operations analysis\nwere divided into four areas; experiment operations, station operations,\nlogistics support, and safety.\n\nIn general, the experiments and their opera-\n\ntions repre sent the Space Station mis sion, while the station operations repre sent the support activities on- orbit which are required for mis sion\naccomplishment.\n\n75\n\n3.2. 1 ExperiITlent Operations\nDetailed descriptions of experiITlent operations with associated tiITlelines at\nthe FPE level are given in Appendix A Station Operations\nSustained operations will be initiated with the third Log M flight five months\nafter the first launch, when the ISS crew is increase\'!. to six men.\n\nISS opera-\n\ntions will continue until four years and seven months later when the Space\nStation crew is increased to 12 men.\n\nOver the entire Space Station program,\n\na total of approximately 275, 000 man-hours of productive operations will be\nprovided on-orbit.\n\nOf these, 88, 000 will be provided during ISS operations.\n\nThe crew will be delivered to orbit as passengers onboard the Space Shuttle.\nOnce docking has been completed, the two crewmen will enter the Shuttle airlock, transferring through the expandable tunnel to the payload pallel interface.\n\nThe crewmen then will equalize the pressure across the Log Module\n\nhatch, open the hatch, and transfer through the Log Module airlock and the\nLog Module to the Space Station.\n\nThe exit of crewmen returning to earth is\n\nthe reverse operation along the same path.\nThe Space Station design approach has paid careful attention to minimizing\ncrew tasks and complexity associated with routine operations and maintenance activities to provide the maximum possible number of man hours onorbit for experiment operations.\n\nThe general crew functions for the on-orbit\n\ncrew include the following station operations:\na.\n\nSubsystem operations.\n\nb.\n\nInventory control.\n\nc.\n\nLogistics vehicle checkout and operations.\n\nd.\n\nResource planning.\n\ne.\n\nStation maintenance.\n\nf.\n\nSafety and medical care.\n\ng.\n\nExperiment interfaces.\n\nh.\n\nOnboard checkout.\n\ni.\n\nContingenc y modification.\n\nAll of the daily routine operations for station operations will be automated to\nthe maximum extent practical.\n\nA typical example is the monitoring of criti-\n\ncal parameters and activation of appropriate warning devices for out -oftolerance conditions.\n\nThe actuation of appropriate response to warnings will\n\nbe a crew responsibility; however, system switching to redundant or backup\n\n94\n\nsysterns will also be automated as much as possible.\n\nDuring each shift, one\n\ncrewman will be assigned the responsibility of the control console located in\nthe Crew Operations Module.\n\nWhen operations are minimal at this position,\n\nthe operator will perform other duties, but he is available if malfunction or\nemergency conditions arise requiring his attention.\n\nAdditionally, the crew will perform the following activities: food preparation\nand cleanup, hygiene and waste management, rest and relaxation, physical\nfitness and exercise, housekeeping (cleanup), and routine medical and dental\ncare.\n\nSince some of the subsystem requirements were changed for the Modular\nSpace Station, it was deemed necessary to reexamine crew requirements for\nstation operations to ensure adequate crewtime to support experiment operations.\n\nPersonal activities were not reexamined in detail since they remained\n\nthe same as for the 33 -it station, and equipment, procedures, and general\nphilosophy regarding crew-equipment interfaces are unchanged for the\nModular Space Station.\nThe major effort for the Modular Space Station crew ope rations analysis was\nto determine the time required for routine operations.\n\nThe time for personal\n\nactivities was defined as 90 min a man per day for eating, hygiene, and waste\nand 8 hr a man per day for sleeping.\n\nThus, the essential objective of the\n\nanalysis was to determine how the crew spends its 10 hr a day of duty time.\n\nTo facilitate the generation and compilation of these data, the form shown in\nFigure 3 -10 was devised.\n\nThe form is based on the premise that all provides the transportation system to deliver and return the\ncrew, RAM\'s, experime.nt equipment, and supplies to the Modular Space\nStation.\n\nAn analysis of crew rotation requirements, Space Station sub-\n\nsystem resupply cargo, and experiment (RAM\'s, equipment, samples,\ndata, and re supply) cargo was accomplished to identify the flight requirements for the ISS and GSS operations.\n\nThe other key factors that were\n\nintegrated with the cargo requirements to establish the flight schedule\nwere:\nA.\n\nThe shuttle delivery and return payload capability is\n20, 000 lb.\n\nB.\n\nThe maximum shuttle launch frequency is every 30 days,\n\nC.\n\nThe shuttle can accommodate a maximum of two\npassengers.\n\nD.\n\nCrew rotation cycles are based on a nominal frequency\nof every 90 days.\n\nE.\n\nThe maximum cargo payload accommodation of a Logistics\nModule is 14, 000 lb.\n\nF.\n\nThe maximum cargo payload capability of a Crew Cargo\nModule is 11, 000 lb.\n\nG.\n\nThe Modular Station initial buildup and on-orbit assembly\nlogistics support will include the delivery of off-loaded\nsubsystem equipment and a large inventory of spa\'res over\nand above the nominal resupply requirements.\n\nH.\n\nResupply cycles are scheduled on nominal 90-day centers.\n\n1.\n\nExperiment Case 534 will be used to establish a flight\nschedule.\n\nThe results of the analysis impacting the ISS\n\nflight schedule include a total cargo weight of 254, 000 Ib\ndelivered to the ISS during five years of operation.\n\n104\n\nA detailed description of station subsystem and experiment cargo\nrequirements including cargo mix of gases, liquids, and solids by weight\nand volume is presented in SE-06.\n\nCargo Handling\nThe on-orbit "pantry conceptI I developed during the 33-ft station study\nwas reevaluated for the modular station de sign and operation.\n\nThe\n\nreevaluation concluded that the pantry concept was the be st concept and\noperational mode for on- orbit storage, inventory control, cargo transfer\n(loading and unloading), and daily utilization by the crew.\n\nTradeoffs\n\nemphasized crew time, storage requirements aboard the station, and\nscheduling of cargo tasks.\n\nThe pantry concept continues to provide the\n\nminimum onboard storage requirements for other station modules, represents the minimum crew time required for cargo transfer, and allows\nthe transfer of cargo on an as-needed basis or noninterference scheduling with experiment operations.\n\nThe requirement for 120 days of supplie s on orbit after each re supply cycle\nis satisfied by having 30 days of operating spares, consumables, and expendables onboard the station and 90 days of supplies in the logistics module.\nWith the arrival of the next logistics flight, the returning Logistics Module\nalso returns the supplie s not utilized plus cargo from the station.\n\nThe new\n\nLogistics Module delivers 90 day s of supplie s and the cycle continue s with\n\n105\n\neach Logistics Module rotation.\n\nAnother approach is to off -load the\n\nremaining supplies not utilized onto the station rather than return all unused\ncargo to ground inventory.\n\nAn analysis of the cargo manifest concludes that\n\nalmost all of the nonutilized items would be spares for the first 11 quarters\nof ISS operations.\n\nThe spares would be added to the inventory and controlled\n\nby the combined on-orbit/ ground inventory control system.\nwould then be void of these items.\n\nFuture flights\n\nThe additional onboard storage for these\n\nitems would be minimal.\nThe remaining quarters (ll to 20) of ISS operations require two Logistics\nModule deliveries for each 90-day phase to satisfy resupply requirements.\nCargo transfer operations will be similar to those developed for the 33 -ft\nstation.\n\nSelected liquids and gases will be transferred by a transfer system\n\ncontrolled from the Space Station com.m.and control console (Figure 3 -12) to\nthe subsystem using points (RCS, RAM, GPL, etc) throughout the station.\nThe fluid or gas will be pumped or gas pres sure -fed to effect transfer.\nEach neuter docking port on the Modular Space Station will have the interface connectors and capability to mate to the Logistic Modules for these\ntransfer operations.\n\nAll cryogenics will be transferred by the crew from\n\nthe Logistics Module to the GPL or RAM.\n\nThe study emphasis on low initial\n\ncost eliminates all large quantities of cryogenics for controlled transfer by\npumping.\n\nCurrent technology has not developed an operational system or\n\ntechnique for cryogenic transfer in zero g.\n\nDDT &E costs to develop a sys-\n\ntem for cryogenic transfer would exceed the study guidelines for low initial\ncost.\n\nThe 33 -ft station study considered developing a system to pump or\n\ntransfer cryogenic by a controlled transfer system.\n\nSmall quantities of\n\nspecial fluids or gases required for experiment operations will be transferred\nthe same as solid cargo.\nSolid cargo transfer will primarily be accomplished by the crew, which will\nhand-control and translate each item on an as -needed basis or periodically\naccording to schedule.\n\nThe majority of solid cargo items can be safely\n\nhandled and transferred by the crew from the Logistics Module or Crew Cargo\nModule to the power, crew, or GPLmodules without the aid of a cargohandling system.\n\nHowever, the characteristics of a few items exceed the\n106\n\n......\n\n\'-J\n\no\n\nFigure 3-12. rations\nModule\nCrew/Operations\nModule\n\nTo operate the system, the crew would attach the cable runs from the LM to\nthe equipment location aisle zone in the station module where the equipment\nor item is to be transferred.\n\nThe crew would then proceed to the storage\n\nrack of the LM and attach one cable tracker to the cable.\n\nThe tracker is\n\nclamped and locked on the cable and is normally spring-loaded to the brake\nposition.\n\nThe crew depresses the trigger mechanism releasing the brake and\n\nthe tracker is free to slide along the cable.\n\nReleasing the trigger auto-\n\nmatically engages the tracker brake.\nWith one tracker installed, the crew would then attach an adjustable cargo\nconstraint cable.\n\nOne end anchors to the tracker and the other end attaches\n\nto the cargo -packaging fitting.\n\nThe tracker and anchor remove the cargo-\n\nholding constraints (bolts, straps, etc), releasing the cargo from its storage\nrack.\n\nThe cargo item is pulled into the aisleway to allow installation of the\n\nsecond tracker, and guide cables are then adjusted to the proper tension to\nconstrain the cargo.\n\nThe crew is now ready to translate the cargo along the\n\ncable run.\nThe crewmen release the tracker brake and the crewmen provide the force\nto translate the item.\n\nThe crew performs the rever se procedure to remove\n\nand install the item at its destination point.\nthe use of the proposed system.\n\nFigure 3 -15 further demonstrates\n\nThe system provides a very simple, easy-\n\nto -operate and store, minimum-maintenance system and cost compared to\nother systems evaluated.\n\n3.3 DUTY CYCLES\nThe mission ground rules which affect the duty schedules are summarized\nas follows:\n\nA.\n\nThe ISS will be operational when fully manned (at least two crewmen), and fully configured, including the GPL and at least two\nRAM\'s.\n\nB.\n\nThe baseline workday is 10 hours, with one day off.\n\nThe work\n\nperiod will be scheduled within a l2-hour tour of duty for seven\ndays a week.\n\n112\n\nW\n\n-\n\nFigure 3-15. Cargo Handling System Components\n\nGUIDE\nCABLES\n\nSPRING-LOADED\nBRAKE TRIGGER\n\nPACKAGE\n\nHOOK\n\nHOOK\n\nR272\n\nC.\n\nFor on-orbit operations, no system or subsystem design will\nprohibit single or dual-shift operations, even though a dual shift\nis currently recommended.\n\nD.\n\nAn on-orbit scheduling technique is used to integrate scheduled\nsubsystem maintenance with experiment operation and maintenance\nto minimize operational interferences among these activities.\n\nThe duty cycle recommended for the ISS is two 12-hour tours or responsibility to provide 24-hour coverage of all operations and still permit considerable choice in the scheduling of activities.\n\nScheduled duty time will\n\nactually consist of no more than 10 hours within the 12-hour tour of responsibility.\n\nDuring the remaining two hours, the crewmen is available if\n\nrequired; that is, he is not asleep or otherwise not readily available.\n\nWith\n\nthis arrangement, only four meal periods are actually required, two of which\ncould be scheduled to bring both shifts together.\nFigure 3-16 depicts a generalized duty cycle for three crewmen who share a\n12 -hour tour of duty.\n\nIt illustrates how three crewmen can be available\n\nconcurrently for those tasks requiring three men and retain the appropriate\nbreaks for hygiene, waste, eating, and off-duty time.\n\nActually, the total\n\nshift is extended to 15.5 hours, which would provide a desirable overlap of\nshift operations.\n\nIt also indicates how individual variation in duty cycling is\n\nattained without comprising any constraints.\nTables 3 -12 through 3 -15 shows the time requirements and scheduling of\nactivities for the four crewmen whose primary responsibility is station\noperation and maintenance.\nshift\n\nop~ration\n\nCrewmen No. 1 and 2 are responsible for first\n\nand crewmen No. 3 and 4 for second shift operations.\n\nThe crewmen can schedule the order of performing tasks to suit the needs of\nthe day or their own personal needs without jeopardizing the operation and\nmaintenance of the station.\n\n114\n\n( Jl\n\n-\n\n3\n\n4\n\n5\n\n6\n7\n\n8\n\n9\n\nI\n\n10\n\n11\n\nDUTY\n\n12\n13\n14\nI\n\n15\n\n16\n\nDUTY\n\nDUTY\n\nDUTY\n\nDUTY\n\nDUTY\n\nEAT\n\nFOOD PREPARATION AND CLEANUP\n\nOFF DUTY: HYGIENE, WASTE. L 2. 2 Meteoroid protection shall be provided by the MSS design\nwhen exposed to the meteoroid flux given in TMX-53865, Second Edition,\nAugust 1970.\n3.2.3.5 The probability of no loss of pressure in a habitable module\nshall be O. 9 or greater during the initial 10 years of Space Station\noperation.\n3.7. 1.3.5.2 Provisions shall be made for detecting, locating, and\nrepairing meteoroid damage.\n\nThe following meteoroid protection features are provided: meteoroid bumper,\ntotal pressure monitor, leak sensors, caution and warning, repair kit, and\ncompartment evacuation precedence (as discus sed earlier).\n\nThese features\n\nare discussed in detail in SE-03, Detailed Preliminary Design.\n3.2. 1. 1. 1. 4 Onboard systems shall be provided for checkout, monitoring\' warning, and fault isolation to a level consistent with safety and with\nthe in-orbit maintenance and repair of failures or damage shall also be\nprovided.\n\nAs a goal, the overall station operations shall not be sub-\n\nstantially degraded by selected repair modes.\n3.2. 1. 1. 23 Space Station systems which incorporate failure -related\nautomatic switch-over controls shall be designed to provide crew notification of the switch-over and conform proper operation of the system\n\n120\n\non-line.\n\nFor critical failures the crew shall be automatically notified\n\nof conditions requiring crew attention.\nOnboard systems are provided with automatic checkout and warning for\ncritical parameters.\n\nThis capability includes automatic visual and oral\n\nnotification, automatic initiation of fault isolation, independent redundant\nsensors, dedicated display, separate power, full backup capability from\nalternate location, and display in each habitable compartment.\n\nWarning parameters include 02 storage, N2 storage, dump and relief valve,\ncontamination monitor, coolant H20 circulation, radiator control, radiator\nrecirculation, propellant, compartment pressure, partial 02, and fire.\n3.2.6.2. I\n\nThe Space Station shall be divided into at least two pressurized.\n\nhabitable volumes so that any damaged module can be isolated as\nrequired.\n\nAccessible modules will be equipped and provisioned so that\n\nthe crew can safely continue a degraded mission and take corrective\naction to either repair or replace the damaged module.\nLong term refuge is provided in either the GPL or the Crew/Operations\nModules.\n\nEach is equipped with an independent EclLS system, cornrnand-\n\ncontrol capability, docking port for logistics, fault detection/ isolation, and\nfood, water, and supplies.\n3.2.6.2.2 Sensors shall be installed to provide fire warnings in sensitive or danger areas.\n\nFire suppressant techniques such as fire\n\nextinguishers or automatic isolation and decompression of module\ncompartments shall be provided.\n\nEvery known precaution will be taken to reduce fire hazards since there is\nno detection, extinguishing and suppressant system to avoid all crew risk.\nSources of ignition will be minimized and use of flarnrnable material will be\nreduced to the least quantity possible.\n\nEgress hatches between modules will\n\nprovide the crew with irnrnediate egress to a safe compartment.\nwill be preinstalled on wires and wire supports.\n\nHard covers\n\nThe electrical system will\n\nincorporate other safety features such as bonding and circuit protection.\n\n121\n\nThese design features will be augmented with emergency lighting, caution\nand warning, etc.\n\nThe OCS will continually monitor critical operations and\n\nalert the crew when a safety problem arises.\n3.2.6.2.6 Pressure hatch design shall provide a means of visual\nverification that proper closure has been accomplished.\n3.7.1. 3. 9. 2 A minimum of two airlocks, each having a two-man capability, shall be provided.\nThree EVA air locks will normally be available to the crew.\nthrough the GPL airlock.\n\nPlanned EVA is\n\nAlternates are located in the Log M and windows in each hatch, and (5) pres sureequalizing valve in hatch.\n3.2.6.3.2 Personnel escape routes shall be provided in all hazardous\nsituations.\n\nA design goal shall be to provide alternate model, the resultant dose is 9.4 rem to the BFO over a 90-day\nperiod.\nThe radiation protection assembly requirements are primarily for monitoring\nand measuring the extent and kind of radiation Rev. 1\n(Greenbook), and subsequent analysis.\n\nThe crew requirements for each\n\nsubgroup and FPE are presented in Appendix A and supercede the Greenbook\nwhere conflicts exist.\n\nFor ease in reference, the crew timelines (Appendix A) have been added to\nthe Operational Sequences submitted as part of task 2.2. I-b.\n\nThe time\n\nrequirements are totaled for each skill and are listed in average manhours\nper day.\n\nA summary of the experiment skill and time requirements is\n\nshown in Table 2.\n\n2. I\n\nGROUND RULES AND ASSUMPTIONS\na)\n\nAll crew times for the subgroups were estimated considering only\nthe requirements of that subgroup.\n\nThe mode of accommodation\n\nand the impact of concurrent operation with other subgroups or\nexperiments have not been considered.\nb)\n\nAll EVA times include three hours prep and post EVA activities.\n\nc)\n\nAt the FPE level, crew times have taken commonality of activities\ninto consideration where several experiments within the FPE could\nbe conducted and monitored concurrently.\n\nWhere sequential opera-\n\ntions are required, the total FPE crew times are the sum of the\nsubgroup requirements.\nd)\n\nEVA is always performed with two men.\n\nThe second man is identi-\n\nfied as "EVA Backup" when specific skill is not COMPOSITION REQUIREMENTS\n\nThe data in Table 5-1 summarizes the crew skill and time requirements for\nthe experiments within each Functional Program Element (FPE).\n\nThe times\n\nlisted for each skill are totals for all tasks including Setup, Operate and\nShutdown/ Evaluate.\n\nThe EVA time requirements include preparation time -and post-EVA activities\nin addition to the time required outs ide the spacecraft.\n\nThe crew s kill requirements indicate a requirement for a particular skill and\ndo not indicate the number of crewmen required.\n\nCrew time per day indicates the time required for each skill during the period of experiment operation.\n\nWhere the experiment operates continually,\n\nthe crew time per day shown is a daily average.\n\nThe crew times and skills\n\nfor both the free flying, attached modules and integral experiments are\nprovided.\n\nThe free flying and attached modules are noted in the remarks\n\ncolumn.\n\n237\n\n00\n\nW\n\nI\\)\n\nAdvanced Stellar
